Layers of Hanafi Scholars and Their Authority
Describes layers of Hanafi jurists and a dispute on ijtihad levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five layers of Hanafi muqallid scholars and explain the dispute over the ijtihad level of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
Not all Hanafi scholars have equal authority; they are ranked by ijtihad level, and even Abu Hanifa's top students are debated.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya introduces the classification of Hanafi jurists into layers, a crucial tool for muftis to weigh legal opinions. It also presents a scholarly dispute about the exact ijtihad level of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, highlighting the dynamic nature of Hanafi legal authority.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Purpose of Classification
The author states that a mufti must know the layers (tabaqat) to place scholars in their proper stations and avoid giving precedence to a lower-ranking scholar over a higher one.
This ensures that legal opinions are derived from the most authoritative sources.
2
Identify the First Layer
The first layer consists of the immediate students of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar, and others. These scholars practice ijtihad within the madhhab, deriving rulings using the four sources but following Abu Hanifa's usul.
Al-Kafawi calls this the 'second layer of ijtihad', indicating it is lower than absolute ijtihad.
3
Identify the Second Layer
The second layer comprises later senior scholars like al-Khassaf, al-Tahawi, al-Karkhi, and Shams al-A'imma. They also practice ijtihad within the madhhab but are later in time.
There are three more layers mentioned in the full passage, but they are not detailed here.
4
Analyze the Dispute on Ijtihad Level
Imam al-Lakhnawi argues that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad are mujtahid mutlaq muntasib (absolute affiliated mujtahids) because they often disagreed with Abu Hanifa on usul. This contradicts Ibn Kamal Pasha's classification of them as mujtahid fi al-madhhab.
The practical consequence: if they are absolute, their opinions can override Abu Hanifa's usul in some cases; if within the school, they must follow his usul strictly.

First Study: The spread of knowledge in Rome, and it gathered people of virtues and masters of sciences, by the blessing of the Ottoman Sultanate. Ended.) (From al-Insaf fi Asbab al-Ikhtilaf, p. 39.) Second Study: Regarding the mention of the layers of our Hanafi companions and their degrees. And this is something that a scholar-mufti must be aware of, to place people in their proper stations, and not to put the lower before the higher. And I have expanded on this in my treatise 'Al-Nafi' al-Kabir li man Yutali' al-Jami' al-Saghir', and in 'Al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyya' and its 'Ta'liqat al-Saniyya', and we mention here a necessary amount with useful additions. So know that al-Kafawi mentioned in 'Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya': 'Indeed the jurists, meaning from the imitating shaykhs, are of five layers: First: The layer of the early ones from our companions, like the students of Abu Hanifa, such as Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and others, may Allah have mercy on them. For indeed they engage in ijtihad within the school, and derive rulings from the four evidences according to the principles established by their teacher Abu Hanifa. For even if they differ with him in some subsidiary rulings, they follow him in the principles of usul, unlike Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal, may Allah have mercy on them; for they differ with him in subsidiary rulings, not following him in usul. And this layer is the second layer of ijtihad. Second: The layer of the senior later ones from the Hanafis, like Abu Bakr Ahmad al-Khassaf, Imam Abu Ja'far Ahmad al-Tahawi, Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, and Shams al-A'imma Abd al-Aziz...' (From al-Nafi' al-Kabir, p. 7; from Kuta'ib A'lam al-Akhyar, etc.) Imam al-Lakhnawi said in al-Nafi' al-Kabir (p. 15): 'It is explicitly stated in many statements that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad are absolute affiliated mujtahids, because their disagreements with the Imam in usul are not few, and this contradicts counting them among the mujtahids within the school. And the apparent is this.' And he said in al-Ta'liqat al-Saniyya (p. 163): 'Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani was counted by Ibn Kamal as among the layer of mujtahids within the school, who do not disagree with their Imam in usul, even if they disagree in some issues. And likewise he counted Abu Yusuf among them. But this is objectionable, because their disagreements with the Imam in usul are many, not few. So the truth is that they are from the affiliated mujtahids.' (He will be mentioned later, and those after him from the companions of this layer and some companions of the following layers... al-Tahawi was counted by Ibn Kamal Pasha...)
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents two introductory studies. The first study briefly notes the spread of Hanafi knowledge through the Ottoman Empire and highlights the role of Abu Yusuf, who as chief judge under Harun al-Rashid, was instrumental in spreading the Hanafi school in Iraq, Khurasan, and Transoxiana. This historical vignette sets the stage for the second, more detailed study on the layers (tabaqat) of Hanafi scholars. The second study is essential for muftis to know the proper hierarchy and ranking of scholars, ensuring that those of lower rank are not given precedence over those of higher rank. The author references his own works for fuller treatment. The core of the passage is a quotation from al-Kafawi's 'Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya' which divides Hanafi muqallid scholars into five layers. The first layer consists of the immediate students of Abu Hanifa—Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar, and others. These scholars perform ijtihad within the madhhab; they derive rulings from the four sources (Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) but strictly follow Abu Hanifa's usul (principles of jurisprudence). Although they may differ from him in subsidiary matters (furu'), they remain faithful to his methodological foundations. This distinguishes them from the founders of other schools, who differ in both furu' and usul. Al-Kafawi calls this the 'second layer of ijtihad,' indicating that ijtihad within the madhhab is of a lower rank than absolute ijtihad. The second layer comprises later senior Hanafi scholars, such as al-Khassaf, al-Tahawi, al-Karkhi, and Shams al-A'imma. These are also mujtahids within the madhhab but belong to a later generation. The footnote from Imam al-Lakhnawi clarifies an important intra-madhhab dispute: whether Abu Yusuf and Muhammad are absolute mujtahids (mujtahid mutlaq) or mujtahids within the school (mujtahid fi al-madhhab). Al-Lakhnawi argues that because they often disagreed with Abu Hanifa on principles (usul), they should be considered affiliated absolute mujtahids (mujtahid mutlaq muntasib). He critiques Ibn Kamal Pasha's classification of them as merely mujtahid fi al-madhhab. The author leaves the reader with this scholarly nuance, underscoring the complexity of Hanafi legal hierarchy.

Key Terms
طبقة (tabaqa): A layer or class of scholars arranged by level of ijtihad and chronological period.
مجتهد في المذهب (mujtahid fi al-madhhab): A scholar who performs ijtihad within the Hanafi school, following its usul while possibly differing in furu'.
مجتهد مطلق منتسب (mujtahid mutlaq muntasib): An absolute mujtahid who is affiliated to the Hanafi school but does not follow its usul fully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first layer of Hanafi scholars described in the passage, how do Abu Yusuf and Muhammad relate to Abu Hanifa's principles (usul)?
A) They completely reject Abu Hanifa's usul and follow their own independent methodology.
B) They follow Abu Hanifa's usul even though they may differ in subsidiary rulings (furu').
C) They only follow Abu Hanifa in furu' but not in usul.
D) They are not considered part of the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school classifies its scholars into layers of ijtihad, and the precise ranking of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad remains a debated but critical issue for determining the authority of their opinions.

Method of Learning

The Five Layers of Hanafi Jurists (Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya)
Classification and dispute over rank

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Hanafi jurists into the five layers based on their ijtihad capability and identify the rank of Imam al-Tahawi with supporting evidence.

Core Idea
Hanafi scholars are hierarchically ranked by ijtihad ability; al-Tahawi belongs to the highest layer, not the intermediate.

This passage introduces the five layers of Hanafi jurists, a crucial taxonomy for any mufti to properly weigh scholarly opinions. It begins by quoting al-Kafawi's Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya, then engages in an important dispute about the rank of Imam al-Tahawi. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the layers and understand why the author considers Tahawi a mujtahid muntasib equal to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five layers
Read the passage and extract the five layers described. Name each layer and list representative scholars.
The first layer are the early students of Abu Hanifa; the second are the great later mujtahids; the third are ashab al-takhrij; the fourth and fifth are not detailed but implied by the hierarchy.
2
Distinguish the criteria for each layer
For each layer, determine the key criterion: the scope of ijtihad (can they oppose the imam in usul? in furu'? can they only derive in cases with no narration?).
The first layer can differ in furu' but not usul; the second can only derive when no narration exists and cannot oppose in either; the third can only derive from the imam's statements.
3
Focus on the third layer and the discussion about al-Tahawi
The passage mentions al-Razi as an example of the third layer, then immediately disputes the placement of al-Tahawi in this layer. Identify the author's argument that Tahawi actually belongs to the first layer.
The author uses evidence from Tahawi's Mukhtasar to show he chose contrary to the madhhab when evidence was strong.
4
Analyse the evidence for al-Tahawi's higher rank
List the evidence cited: Tahawi's Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar and his Mukhtasar contain rulings that diverge from Abu Hanifa's positions. Also note the support from al-Dihlawi's statement.
The author concludes that Tahawi is a mujtahid muntasib, following Abu Hanifa's method but not his specific rulings.
5
Conclude with the practical implication for fatwa
Determine how this classification affects a mufti's practice: Tahawi's opinions carry independent weight and can be preferred when evidence supports them, unlike opinions from the third layer which must conform to the madhhab.
The thamarah al-khilaf is that Tahawi's rulings are not mere re-statements of the school but have ijtihadi authority.

Source Text

الدراسة الثانية
في ذكر طبقات أصحابنا الحنفية ودرجاتهم
وهذا أمر لا بد للعالم المفتي من الاطلاع عليه لينزل الناس منازلهم، ولا يقدّم أدناهم على أعلاهم، وقد بسطت الكلام فيه في رسالتي «النافع الكبير لمن يطالع الجامع الصغير» ، وفي «الفوائد البهية» و«تعليقاتها السنية»، ونذكر هاهنا قدراً ضرورياً مع زيادات مفيدة.
فاعلم أنه ذكر الكفوي في «طبقات الحنفية» : «إنّ الفقهاء يعني من المشايخ المقلدين على خمس طبقات : الأولى : طبقة المتقدمين من أصحابنا كتلاميذ أبي حنيفة له نحو أبي يوسف ومحمد وزفر وغيرهم، فإنّهم يجتهدون في المذهب، ويستخرجون الأحكام عن الأدلة الأربعة على حسب القواعد التي قررها أُستاذهم أبو حنيفة. فإنّهم وإن خالفوه في بعض أحكام الفروع لكنّهم يقلدونه في قواعد الأصول، بخلاف مالك والشافعي وابن حنبل؛ فإنهم يخالفونه في أحكام الفروع ، غير مقلدين له في الأصول، وهذه الطبقة هي الطبقة الثانية من الاجتهاد.
والثانية : طبقة أكابر المتأخرين من الحنفية كأبي بكر أحمد الخصاف، والإمام أبي جعفر أحمد الطحاوي، وأبي الحسن الكرخي، وشمس الأئمة عبد العزيز الحَلْوَانِي، وشمس الأئمة محمّد السَّرَخْسِي، وفخر الإسلام عليّ بن محمّد البَزْدَويّ، والإمام فخر الدين حسن المعروف بقاضي خان، والصدر الأجل برهان الدين محمود، صاحب «الذخيرة البرهانية»، و«المحيط البرهاني»، والشيخ طاهر بن أحمد صاحب «النصاب» و«الخلاصة»، وأمثالهم. فإنهم يقدرون على الاجتهاد في المسائل التي لا رواية فيها عن صاحب المذهب، ولا يقدرون على المخالفة له لا في الأصول، ولا في الفروع، ولكنهم يستنبطونها على حسب أصول قررها ، ومقتضى قواعد بسطها صاحب المذهب.
الثالثة : طبقة أصحاب التخريج . المقلدين ؛ كالرازي وغيره من طبقة من يقدر على الاجتهاد في المسائل التي لا رواية فيها، ولا يقدر على مخالفة صاحب المذهب لا في الفروع، ولا في الأصول، وهو منظور فيه ؛ فإنَّ له درجة عالية، ورتبة شامخة، قد خالف بها صاحب المذهب في كثير من الأصول والفروع، ومن طالع «شرح معاني الآثار»، وغيره من مصنفاته يجده يختار خلاف ما اختارَهُ صاحب المذهب كثيراً ، إذا كان ما يدل عليه قوياً.
فالحق أنه من المجتهدين المنتسبين الذين ينتسبون إلى إمام معين من المجتهدين ، لكن لا يقلدونه لا في الفروع ، ولا في الأصول، لكونهم متصفين بالاجتهاد، وما انتسبوا إليه إلا لسلوكهم طريقه في الاجتهاد.
وإن انحط عن ذلك، فهو من المجتهدين في المذهب القادرين على استخراج الأحكام من القواعد التي قررها الإمام، ولا تنحط مرتبته عن هذه المرتبة أبدا على رغم أنف من جعلهُ منحطاً، وما أحسن كلام المولى عبد العزيز المحدّث الدَّهْلَوِيّ في «بستان المحدِّثِينَ»، حيث قال ما معربه : إِنَّ «مختصر الطحاوي) يدلُّ على أنه كان مجتهداً ، ولم يكن مقلداً للمذهب الحنفي تقليداً محضاً، فإنه اختار فيه أشياء تخالف مذهب أبي حنيفة لما لاح له من الأدلة القوية. انتهى.
وفي الجملة فهو في طبقة أبي يوسف ومحمد ، لا ينحط عن مرتبتهما على القول المسدَّدِ».
The Second Study: On Mentioning the Layers of Our Hanafi Companions and Their Degrees. This is something that the scholar-mufti must be aware of, so that he may place people in their proper stations and not advance the lower over the higher. I have expanded the discussion in my treatise 'al-Nafi' al-Kabir li man Yutali' al-Jami' al-Saghir', and in 'al-Fawa'id al-Bahiyya' and its 'Ta'liqat al-Saniyya'. Here we mention an essential amount with useful additions. Know that al-Kafawi mentioned in 'Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya': 'The jurists, meaning from the shaykhs who are muqallids, are of five layers: The first layer: the early ones from our companions, like the students of Abu Hanifa such as Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar and others. For they exercise ijtihad in the madhhab and derive rulings from the four proofs according to the principles established by their teacher Abu Hanifa. Even if they differ with him in some subsidiary rulings, they follow him in the principles of usul. Unlike Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ibn Hanbal; they differ with him in subsidiary rulings, not following him in usul. This layer is the second level of ijtihad. The second layer: the great later ones from the Hanafis, such as Abu Bakr Ahmad al-Khassaf, Imam Abu Ja'far Ahmad al-Tahawi, Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, Shams al-A'imma 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Halwani, Shams al-A'imma Muhammad al-Sarakhsi, Fakhr al-Islam 'Ali ibn Muhammad al-Bazdawi, Imam Fakhr al-Din Hasan known as Qadi Khan, al-Sadr al-Ajal Burhan al-Din Mahmud, author of 'al-Dhakhira al-Burhaniyya' and 'al-Muhit al-Burhani', and Shaykh Tahir ibn Ahmad, author of 'al-Nisab' and 'al-Khulasa', and their likes. They are capable of ijtihad in matters for which there is no narration from the founder of the madhhab, but they cannot oppose him in usul or furu'; rather they derive rulings according to the principles he established and the rules he laid out. The third layer: the layer of the people of takhrij, the muqallids, like al-Razi and others from a layer who are capable of ijtihad in matters with no narration, and cannot oppose the founder in furu' or usul. But there is a discussion about him; indeed he has a high degree and exalted rank, and he has opposed the founder in many usul and furu'. Whoever studies his 'Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar' and other works finds him often choosing contrary to what the founder chose, when the evidence indicated it strongly. So the truth is that he is among the mujtahidun muntasibun, who affiliate to a specific imam among the mujtahids, but do not follow him in furu' or usul, because they are characterized by ijtihad; they affiliate to him only because they follow his method in ijtihad. And if one is lower than that, he is among the mujtahidun fi al-madhhab, capable of extracting rulings from the principles established by the Imam, and his rank never falls below this level, despite anyone who claims otherwise. How excellent is the statement of Mawla 'Abd al-'Aziz al-Muhaddith al-Dihlawi in 'Bustan al-Muhaddithin', where he said in paraphrase: 'Indeed, al-Tahawi's Mukhtasar indicates that he was a mujtahid, not a mere blind follower of the Hanafi madhhab; he chose in it things that contradict the Hanafi madhhab because strong evidence appeared to him.' End. In summary, he is in the layer of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, not lower than their rank on the soundest opinion.'
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification text. It opens by stating the necessity for a scholar-mufti to know the ranks of Hanafi jurists to give proper respect. The author mentions his own works on the subject. He then quotes al-Kafawi's 'Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya', which divides the muqallid scholars into five layers. The first layer consists of the early students of Abu Hanifa (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar). They exercise ijtihad within the madhhab, deriving rulings from the four proofs (Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) based on Abu Hanifa's usul. They may differ in furu' (subsidiary rulings) but not in usul. This distinguishes them from founders of other schools like Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad, who differed in both. The second layer includes later great Hanafis like al-Khassaf, al-Tahawi, al-Karkhi, etc. They can perform ijtihad only in cases with no transmitted ruling from the founder, but cannot oppose him in usul or furu'; they derive rulings analogically from his principles. The third layer are 'ashab al-takhrij' (those capable of deriving from the founder's statements) like al-Razi. The author then engages in a discussion about al-Tahawi's rank, arguing that he actually belongs to the higher layer of mujtahidun muntasibun (mujtahids who follow a specific imam's methodology but not his rulings), because his works show he often chose different rulings when evidence supported them. The author supports this with al-Dihlawi's statement that Tahawi's Mukhtasar proves his ijtihad. Thus Tahawi is on par with Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Key Terms
طبقات (tabaqat): Layers or levels of scholars according to their capability and authority in ijtihad
المجتهدون المنتسبون (al-mujtahidun al-muntasibun): Mujtahids who attribute themselves to a specific imam (like Abu Hanifa) and follow his methodology but exercise independent ijtihad and may differ in rulings
أصحاب التخريج (ashab al-takhrij): Those capable of deriving rulings from the founder's statements and principles, but cannot oppose him

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author consider Imam al-Tahawi to be in the layer of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rather than in the third layer?
A) Because Tahawi never differed from Abu Hanifa
B) Because Tahawi's Mukhtasar shows he chose rulings contradicting the Hanafi madhhab when evidence was strong
C) Because Tahawi was a student of Abu Hanifa directly
D) Because Tahawi wrote a commentary on al-Quduri

Key Takeaway
The five layers of Hanafi jurists help a mufti determine the authority of different scholarly opinions; al-Tahawi's rank as a mujtahid muntasib means his independent rulings carry significant weight.

Classification of Mujtahids: Independent and Affiliated
Definition of mujtahid levels from Imam Nawawi's classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions and conditions of the independent mujtahid and the four categories of the affiliated mujtahid, and distinguish their levels of ijtihad.

Core Idea
Ijtihad is a graded hierarchy; only the independent mujtahid derives directly from sources, while affiliated mujtahids operate within the madhhab framework.

This passage from Imam al-Nawawi provides a foundational classification of mujtahids. It distinguishes between the fully independent mujtahid (mustaqill) and the affiliated mujtahid (muntasib), who is further divided into four levels. Understanding these categories is essential for recognizing who is qualified to derive rulings and the limits of their authority within the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two broad categories
The passage divides mujtahids into mustaqill (independent) and muntasib (affiliated). Note the conditions for the independent mujtahid: fiqh of the self, sound mind, intellectual discipline, correct derivation, vigilance, knowledge of evidences and their tools, mastery of major issues. The author states this type has been absent for long ages.
The mustaqill is the only one who directly engages with Qur'an and Sunnah without adherence to a specific madhhab.
2
Analyze the four levels of muntasib
List each level: First: One who does not taqlid of his imam in evidence but follows his path—effectively independent but attributed. Second: Mujtahid muqayyad (restricted) within the madhhab, using the imam's principles for qiyas; lacks some tools (e.g., grammar/hadith). Third: Below ashab al-wujuh; a hafiz of the madhhab who edits and prefers rulings but does not derive new ones. Fourth (implicitly): Those who wrongly claim ijtihad and are criticized.
The second level corresponds to ashab al-wujuh in Hanafi tabaqat.
3
Understand the restrictions of the muntasib
Even the highest muntasib is not free from taqlid of the imam. Their ijtihad is based on the transmitted speech of the imams, not directly on Qur'an and Sunnah. This is justified because imams were experts in Shari'ah and Arabic.
The objection that imams' sayings are not infallible is answered: they are not placed equal to revelation but are authoritative due to their expertise.
4
Evaluate the critique of false ijtihad
The author criticizes those who overstep their rank, claiming to present hidden evidences and preferring their own taqlid over that of earlier scholars. This caused great harm and necessitated a sharp response to defend the madhhab.
This reflects the Hanafi concern for preserving the methodological integrity of the school.

وثانيها : أن يكون مجتهداً مقيداً في المذهب، مستقلاً بتقرير أصوله بالدليل، غير أنه لا يتجاوز في أدلة أصول إمامه وقواعده، وشرطه كونه عالماً بالفقه وأصوله وأدلة الأحكام تفصيلاً، وكونه بصيرا بمسالك الأقيسة والمعاني، تام الارتياض في التخريج والاستنباط ؛ لقياس غير المنصوص عليه ؛ لعلمه بأصول إمامه، ولا يعرى عن تقليد له ، لإخلاله ببعض أدوات المستقل ؛ كالنحو والحديث ، وهذه صفة أصحابنا أصحاب الوجوه.
وثالثها : أن لا يبلغ رتبة الوجوه، لكنّه فقية حافظ مذهب إمامه، قائم بتقرير أدلته ، يصور ويحرِّرُ ويقررُ ويمهد ويزيفُ ويرجّح، وهذه صفة كثير من المتأخرين إلى أواخر المئة الرابعة الذين رتبوا المذهب وحرّروا (۳).
فغلطوا في ذلك ، ثم أكثروا في الكلام من نحو هذه الخرافات والمجازفات، وسولت له نفسه أنه أبرز أدلة خفيت عليهم، وأن تقليده أو تقليد شيخه التابع له في الحل أولى من تقليدهم، فلعظيم ضرر . هذه الحادثة وسوء صنيع وطوية هذا المجازف جردت صارم العزم وباتر التنقيب والفحص والفهم، وأوريت زند الفكر حمية لأئمتنا غيوث الهدى ومصابيح الدجى......(۱) هو شارح «صحیح مسلم» يحيى بن شرف النووي المتوفى سنة (٦٧٧) أو سنة (٦٧٦). منه رحمه الله.
(۲) ومصدر الاجتهاد الوحيد عند هذه الطبقة كما فصلته في المدخل ( ص ٢٢٣) هو : «ما نقل إليهم من كلام أئمة المذهب الذين يقلدون أهله»، قال الإمام النووي الشافعي في «المجموع»(١ : ٧٦) ، والإمام المرداوي الحنبلي (ت ٨٨٥هـ) في «الإنصاف» (١٢ : ٢٦٠): «يتخذ نـصـوص إمـامـه أصولا يستنبط منها كفعل المستقل بنصوص الشرع». وإن اعترض عليهم بأن أقوال الأئمة غير معصومة فكيف تنزل منزلة الوحيين المعصومين ؛ لأن ما روي عن الإمام صاحب المذهب ليس

From al-Nawawi (1): 'Indeed the mujtahid is either: (a) an independent mujtahid (mujtahid mustaqill): its conditions include fiqh of the self, soundness of mind, exercise of thought, correctness of action and derivation, alertness, knowledge of the evidences and their tools mentioned in usul and their conditions, and deriving from them with knowledge and practice in their use, and with understanding and mastery of the major issues of the madhhab. This has been absent for long ages. (b) Or an affiliated mujtahid (mujtahid muntasib): and it is four divisions: First: that he does not taqlid of his imam in the madhhab and the evidence, because he is described with the quality of the independent, but rather is attributed to him because he follows his path in ijtihad. Second: that he be a restricted mujtahid (mujtahid muqayyad) in the madhhab, independent in establishing its principles with evidence, but he does not go beyond the evidences of his imam's principles and rules. His condition is that he is knowledgeable in fiqh and its usul and the detailed evidences of rulings, and that he is insightful in the paths of analogies and meanings, fully practiced in takhrij and istinbat, to analogize that which is not explicitly stated, because of his knowledge of his imam's principles. He is not free from taqlid of him, due to his deficiency in some tools of the independent, like grammar and hadith. This is the description of our companions, the ashab al-wujuh. Third: that he does not reach the rank of the wujuh, but he is a faqih, a memorizer of his imam's madhhab, one who undertakes to establish its evidences, describing, editing, deciding, preparing, refuting, and giving preference. This is the description of many of the later scholars up to the end of the 4th century who organized the madhhab and edited it. (3) They erred in that, then they increased speech of such nonsense and reckless statements, and his soul tempted him that he presented evidences hidden from them, and that his taqlid or the taqlid of his shaykh who follows him in permissibility is better than their taqlid. Due to the great harm of this incident and the evil action and intention of this reckless one, I unsheathed the sharp determination and the cutting investigation, examination, and understanding, and kindled the spark of thought out of zeal for our imams, the rains of guidance and the lamps of darkness... (1) He is the commentator of Sahih Muslim, Yahya ibn Sharaf al-Nawawi, died 677 or 676 AH. From him, may Allah have mercy on him. (2) The sole source of ijtihad for this layer, as I detailed in al-Madkhal (p. 223), is: 'What is transmitted to them from the speech of the imams of the madhhab whom they follow.' Imam al-Nawawi al-Shafi'i said in al-Majmu' (1:76), and Imam al-Mardawi al-Hanbali (d. 885 AH) in al-Insaf (12:260): 'He takes the texts of his imam as principles from which he derives, like the action of the independent from the texts of the Shar''. And if it is objected against them that the sayings of the imams are not infallible, so how are they placed in the place of the two infallible revelations? Because what is narrated from the imam, the founder of the madhhab, is not Qur'an, nor authentic hadiths; so how are rulings derived from it? It is answered as follows: It is the speech of mujtahid imams, knowledgeable in the rules of Shari'ah and Arabic, explaining the legal rulings. So its meaning...

Commentary
This passage is a direct quotation from Imam al-Nawawi (d. 676 AH), a leading Shafi'i scholar, on the classification of mujtahids. However, the author of this supercommentary ('Umadat al-Ri'aya) uses it to define the levels of ijtihad within the Hanafi school and to critique those who wrongly claim ijtihad. The classification distinguishes between two broad types: (1) al-mujtahid al-mustaqill (independent mujtahid), who possesses all tools of ijtihad and derives rulings directly from the Qur'an and Sunnah without adhering to any particular madhhab. Nawawi states this type has been absent for a long time. (2) al-mujtahid al-muntasib (affiliated mujtahid), who operates within a madhhab. He divides this into four categories. The first category is one who does not perform taqlid of his imam in the sense of blind following, but rather follows his method; he is essentially independent but attributed to the madhhab because he walks in its path. The second category is the mujtahid muqayyad (restricted mujtahid) within the madhhab, who uses his imam's principles as a framework but can derive rulings for new cases using qiyas, etc. He lacks some tools like grammar and hadith, so he relies on his imam. The third category is below the level of ashab al-wujuh; he is a hafiz (memorizer) of the madhhab and can defend and prefer rulings. The author then criticizes those who overstep by claiming ijtihad without qualifications, leading to errors. The footnotes add that for this category, the source of ijtihad is the transmitted speech of the imams, and they address the objection that imams' sayings are not infallible by arguing that these imams were experts in Shari'ah and Arabic, thus their speech has authority.

Key Terms
مجتهد مستقل (mujtahid mustaqill): An independent mujtahid who derives rulings directly from the primary sources (Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma', Qiyas) without adhering to any particular madhhab.
مجتهد منتسب (mujtahid muntasib): A mujtahid who is affiliated with a particular madhhab and operates within its framework, though they may derive rulings using its principles.
أصحاب الوجوه (ashab al-wujuh): Literally 'people of faces'; these are mujtahids within the madhhab who can derive rulings for new cases using the principles of their imam, but they lack full independent tools.
تقليد (taqlid): Following the ruling of a qualified mujtahid without requiring proof from primary sources. In this context, it refers to the limitation of the affiliated mujtahid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Nawawi's classification, what distinguishes the third category of mujtahid muntasib from the second?
A) The third category is fully independent and does not need taqlid
B) The third category cannot derive new rulings and only memorizes and defends the madhhab
C) The third category is a layperson who must follow any mufti
D) The third category denies the authority of previous imams

Key Takeaway
The classification of mujtahids into mustaqill and muntasib with hierarchical levels preserves the integrity of ijtihad and prevents unqualified individuals from distorting the Shari'ah.

The Fourth Category of Mujtahid: Weak in Proof, Strong in Transmission
Defines the fourth category of mujtahid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of the fourth category of mujtahid in the Hanafi classification and explain the validity of his transmission and fatwa.

Core Idea
Even a weak mujtahid's transmission and fatwa are binding for his followers.

The Hanafi school classifies mujtahids into categories based on their ability. The fourth category describes a scholar who has mastered the madhhab's rulings and can transmit them accurately, but lacks the skill to formulate proofs and analogies independently. This lesson clarifies his status and the authority of his rulings for those who follow him.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Read the passage and locate the description of the fourth category. Note the two qualities: (1) he preserves and transmits the madhhab, understands its difficulties; (2) he is weak in presenting proofs and formulating analogies.
The text says: 'he undertakes to preserve the madhhab and transmit it, understand its difficulties, but he is weak in presenting its proof and formulating its analogies'.
2
Determine the authority of his rulings
Despite his weakness, the passage states his transmission and fatwa are accepted. Why? Because he reliably reports the madhhab's established rulings, even if he cannot derive them himself.
The text says: 'so his transmission and fatwa are considered in what he reports from the recorded issues of his madhhab'.
3
Compare with higher categories
Contrast this fourth category with the higher ones: a mujtahid must have principles (qawa'id). This category lacks independent derivation but still serves as a reliable transmitter.
The next part of the passage explains that even such a mujtahid must have qawa'id, but here the focus is on his role as a transmitter.

الدراسة الثانية طبقات الحنفية

ورابعها : أن يقوم بحفظ المذهب ونقله، وفهم مشكله، ولكنه ضعيف في تقرير دليله، وتحرير أقيسته، فهذا يعتبر نقله وفتواه فيما يحكيه من مسطورات مذهبه». انتهى ملخصاً (۲).
كلامهم حجة على من قلدهم، منطوقاً كان أو مفهوماً صريحاً كان أو إشارة، فكلامهم بالنسبة له كالقرآن والحديث بالنسبة لجميع المجتهدين. وله فضل عظيم لا يستطيع أحد إنكاره ، وهو أنه فتح باباً واسعاً لتطور الفقه ومسايرته لأحداث الحياة» ينظر: «الموسوعة الفقهية المصرية» (۱ : ٢٠).

٢. إنه لا يكون اجتهاد مجتهد إلا بأن يكون له قواعد يحتكم إليها في استخراج الأحكام الفقهية ، سواء كان هو واضعها أو قلّد فيها غيره ؛ لأن استنباط الأحكام الفقهية من الكتاب والسنة يحتاج إلى أصول وقواعد، فمثلاً : إذا تعارضت الأحاديث في الدلالة على حكم من الأحكام يستطيع بالقواعد التي يمشي عليها أن يستخرجه. إذا تقرر هذا فإنه يمكن القول بأن كلام المجتهد في المسائل الفقهية هو تطبيق لقواعده وأصوله التي اعتمدها في استخراج الأحكام، ففي اعتماد حكمه قطع لمرحلة طويلة وصعبة جدا من استنباط للحكم من الأدلة التفصيلية. فالأمـر أمـر مـرحلـية وتدرج، وليس إهمالاً وتركاً للأدلة الشرعية ؛ لأن أحكام المجتهد مأخوذة من الأدلة، فهي تمثلها، ولكنها قطعت مرحلة للمجتهد في المذهب لاستخراج الأحكام التي لم يبينها المجتهد.
وتأكيد هذا ما يلاحظ في القواعد الفقهية التي استخرجت من مجموعة الأحكام الفقهية المتناثرة المتفقة فيما بينهما، ومن ثم يمكن الاعتماد على القاعدة في معرفة الأحكام غير المبينة كما هو معلوم. ينظر : «المنهج الفقهي للإمام اللكنوي»(ص١٤٨ - ١٤٩).
(۱) هذا التقسيم لطبقات المجتهدين ارتضاه الإمام اللكنوي بعد أن نقض التقسيم السابق، وكذلك قبله الإمام الكوثري في حسن التقاضي» (ص (٢٤) ، وهو الأحرى بالقبول، والله أعلم. (۲) من المجموع شرح المهذب»(۱ : ۷۷).
The second study: Categories of Hanafis
And the fourth: that he undertakes to preserve the madhhab and transmit it, understand its difficulties, but he is weak in presenting its proof and formulating its analogies; so his transmission and fatwa are considered in what he reports from the recorded issues of his madhhab. It ends summarized.
Their words are proof (hujja) upon those who follow them, whether explicit or understood, clear or indicative, so their words for him are like the Quran and Hadith for all mujtahids. He has great virtue that no one can deny, which is that he opened a wide door for the development of fiqh and its alignment with life events. Reference: Al-Mawsu'a al-Fiqhiyya al-Misriyya (1:20).
2. It is that there is no ijtihad of a mujtahid unless he has principles to which he refers in extracting fiqh rulings, whether he is their originator or follows others in them; because deriving fiqh rulings from the Book and Sunnah requires principles and rules. For example: when hadiths conflict in indicating a ruling, he can extract it by the principles he follows. If this is established, then it can be said that the speech of a mujtahid in fiqh issues is an application of his principles and methodology that he relied upon in extracting rulings. So, in adopting his ruling, a long and very difficult stage of deriving the ruling from detailed evidences is cut short. It is a matter of stages and progression, not neglect and abandonment of Shari'a evidences; because the rulings of the mujtahid are taken from the evidences, so they represent them, but they cut a stage for the mujtahid in the madhhab to extract rulings that the primary mujtahid did not explain.
Confirmation of this is observed in the fiqh principles (qawa'id) that were extracted from the scattered fiqh rulings that agree among themselves, and then one can rely on the principle in knowing unstated rulings as is known. Reference: Al-Manhaj al-Fiqhi li al-Imam al-Laknawi (pp. 148-149).
Footnote (1): This division of the categories of mujtahids was accepted by Imam al-Laknawi after refuting the previous division, and similarly before him Imam al-Kawthari in 'Husn al-Taqadi' (p. 24), and it is more worthy of acceptance, and Allah knows best. Footnote (2): From 'Al-Majmu' Sharh al-Muhadhdhab' (1:77).
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth category of mujtahid in the Hanafi classification: one who has memorized and transmitted the madhhab, understands its difficult points, but is weak in formulating proofs and analogies. Despite this weakness, his transmission of the madhhab's rulings and his fatwa based on those transmitted rulings are accepted. The author then emphasizes the importance of principles (qawa'id) in ijtihad. Without a systematic methodology, a scholar cannot resolve conflicts among evidences. This does not mean abandoning the primary sources; rather, the rulings of the earlier mujtahids already embody those principles, and later scholars rely on them. The passage concludes by citing that fiqh principles have been extracted from scattered rulings, and they serve to determine rulings not explicitly stated.

Key Terms
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): A jurist capable of deriving rulings; here classified into categories based on ability level.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for accepting the fatwa of a fourth-category mujtahid?
A) He can formulate strong analogies.
B) He reliably transmits the established rulings of the madhhab.
C) He has direct access to revelation.
D) His personal opinion is considered authoritative.

Key Takeaway
A fourth-category mujtahid is a reliable transmitter of the madhhab, and his transmission is binding on followers even though he cannot independently prove rulings.

Why Ijtihad Requires Principles (Qawa'id)
Explains the necessity of qawa'id for ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a mujtahid must have principles (qawa'id) and how they facilitate deriving rulings without abandoning primary sources.

Core Idea
Qawa'id are essential for resolving conflicts and deriving rulings; they are shortcuts, not replacements for primary sources.

The passage argues that no ijtihad is possible without principles (qawa'id) to which the mujtahid refers. These principles are not arbitrary but are derived from scattered rulings and represent the spirit of the Shari'a. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the necessity of qawa'id and addresses a common misconception that they replace the Quran and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Read the passage: 'It is that there is no ijtihad of a mujtahid unless he has principles to which he refers in extracting fiqh rulings.' This is the central claim.
The claim is absolute: no ijtihad without principles.
2
Understand the justification
Why? Because deriving rulings from the Book and Sunnah requires principles and rules. The passage gives an example: when hadiths conflict, the mujtahid needs principles to resolve the conflict.
Principles are a methodological necessity.
3
Address the misconception
The passage stresses that relying on principles does not mean neglecting the primary sources. Rather, the rulings of earlier mujtahids embody the principles, and later scholars apply those principles to new cases.
The text says: 'It is a matter of stages and progression, not neglect and abandonment of Shari'a evidences'.
4
Confirm with qawa'id
The passage notes that qawa'id have been extracted from scattered rulings and can be used to determine rulings not explicitly stated. This is the practical utility.
Qawa'id are inductive generalizations from the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدراسة الثانية طبقات الحنفية

ورابعها : أن يقوم بحفظ المذهب ونقله، وفهم مشكله، ولكنه ضعيف في تقرير دليله، وتحرير أقيسته، فهذا يعتبر نقله وفتواه فيما يحكيه من مسطورات مذهبه». انتهى ملخصاً (۲).
كلامهم حجة على من قلدهم، منطوقاً كان أو مفهوماً صريحاً كان أو إشارة، فكلامهم بالنسبة له كالقرآن والحديث بالنسبة لجميع المجتهدين. وله فضل عظيم لا يستطيع أحد إنكاره ، وهو أنه فتح باباً واسعاً لتطور الفقه ومسايرته لأحداث الحياة» ينظر: «الموسوعة الفقهية المصرية» (۱ : ٢٠).
6
٢. إنه لا يكون اجتهاد مجتهد إلا بأن يكون له قواعد يحتكم إليها في استخراج الأحكام الفقهية ، سواء كان هو واضعها أو قلّد فيها غيره ؛ لأن استنباط الأحكام الفقهية من الكتاب والسنة يحتاج إلى أصول وقواعد، فمثلاً : إذا تعارضت الأحاديث في الدلالة على حكم من الأحكام يستطيع بالقواعد التي يمشي عليها أن يستخرجه. إذا تقرر هذا فإنه يمكن القول بأن كلام المجتهد في المسائل الفقهية هو تطبيق لقواعده وأصوله التي اعتمدها في استخراج الأحكام، ففي اعتماد حكمه قطع لمرحلة طويلة وصعبة جدا من استنباط للحكم من الأدلة التفصيلية. فالأمـر أمـر مـرحلـية وتدرج، وليس إهمالاً وتركاً للأدلة الشرعية ؛ لأن أحكام المجتهد مأخوذة من الأدلة، فهي تمثلها، ولكنها قطعت مرحلة للمجتهد في المذهب لاستخراج الأحكام التي لم يبينها المجتهد.
وتأكيد هذا ما يلاحظ في القواعد الفقهية التي استخرجت من مجموعة الأحكام الفقهية المتناثرة المتفقة فيما بينهما، ومن ثم يمكن الاعتماد على القاعدة في معرفة الأحكام غير المبينة كما هو معلوم. ينظر : «المنهج الفقهي للإمام اللكنوي»(ص١٤٨ - ١٤٩).
(۱) هذا التقسيم لطبقات المجتهدين ارتضاه الإمام اللكنوي بعد أن نقض التقسيم السابق، وكذلك قبله الإمام الكوثري في حسن التقاضي» (ص (٢٤) ، وهو الأحرى بالقبول، والله أعلم. (۲) من المجموع شرح المهذب»(۱ : ۷۷).
The second study: Categories of Hanafis
And the fourth: that he undertakes to preserve the madhhab and transmit it, understand its difficulties, but he is weak in presenting its proof and formulating its analogies; so his transmission and fatwa are considered in what he reports from the recorded issues of his madhhab. It ends summarized.
Their words are proof (hujja) upon those who follow them, whether explicit or understood, clear or indicative, so their words for him are like the Quran and Hadith for all mujtahids. He has great virtue that no one can deny, which is that he opened a wide door for the development of fiqh and its alignment with life events. Reference: Al-Mawsu'a al-Fiqhiyya al-Misriyya (1:20).
2. It is that there is no ijtihad of a mujtahid unless he has principles to which he refers in extracting fiqh rulings, whether he is their originator or follows others in them; because deriving fiqh rulings from the Book and Sunnah requires principles and rules. For example: when hadiths conflict in indicating a ruling, he can extract it by the principles he follows. If this is established, then it can be said that the speech of a mujtahid in fiqh issues is an application of his principles and methodology that he relied upon in extracting rulings. So, in adopting his ruling, a long and very difficult stage of deriving the ruling from detailed evidences is cut short. It is a matter of stages and progression, not neglect and abandonment of Shari'a evidences; because the rulings of the mujtahid are taken from the evidences, so they represent them, but they cut a stage for the mujtahid in the madhhab to extract rulings that the primary mujtahid did not explain.
Confirmation of this is observed in the fiqh principles (qawa'id) that were extracted from the scattered fiqh rulings that agree among themselves, and then one can rely on the principle in knowing unstated rulings as is known. Reference: Al-Manhaj al-Fiqhi li al-Imam al-Laknawi (pp. 148-149).
Footnote (1): This division of the categories of mujtahids was accepted by Imam al-Laknawi after refuting the previous division, and similarly before him Imam al-Kawthari in 'Husn al-Taqadi' (p. 24), and it is more worthy of acceptance, and Allah knows best. Footnote (2): From 'Al-Majmu' Sharh al-Muhadhdhab' (1:77).
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth category of mujtahid in the Hanafi classification: one who has memorized and transmitted the madhhab, understands its difficult points, but is weak in formulating proofs and analogies. Despite this weakness, his transmission of the madhhab's rulings and his fatwa based on those transmitted rulings are accepted. The author then emphasizes the importance of principles (qawa'id) in ijtihad. Without a systematic methodology, a scholar cannot resolve conflicts among evidences. This does not mean abandoning the primary sources; rather, the rulings of the earlier mujtahids already embody those principles, and later scholars rely on them. The passage concludes by citing that fiqh principles have been extracted from scattered rulings, and they serve to determine rulings not explicitly stated.

Key Terms
قواعد فقهية (qawa‘id fiqhiyyah): General principles derived from scattered fiqh rulings that help in extracting new rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between a mujtahid's principles (qawa'id) and the primary sources (Quran and Sunnah)?
A) The principles replace the primary sources in the madhhab.
B) The principles are derived from the primary sources and represent them.
C) The principles are independent of the primary sources and may contradict them.
D) The principles are used only when primary sources are silent.

Key Takeaway
Principles (qawa'id) are indispensable for ijtihad; they streamline derivation without severing the connection to the primary sources.

Three Categories of Hanafi Masail
Classification of Hanafi masail into three layers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three categories of Hanafi masail and explain why Zahir al-Riwayah is the most authoritative.

Core Idea
Not all Hanafi rulings are equal – Zahir al-Riwayah is the foundation.

The Hanafi school classifies its transmitted rulings into three layers: Masail al-Usul (the foundational issues known as Zahir al-Riwayah), Masail al-Nawadir (rare transmissions), and Masail al-Fatawa (later fatwas). This lesson, based on al-Kafawi's classification, focuses on the first and most authoritative category, explaining its scope, key books, and why it forms the primary reference for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
According to al-Kafawi, the issues of the Hanafi madhhab are divided into three tabaqat (layers): Usul, Nawadir, and Fatawa. The passage elaborates only on the first layer.
The other two layers are not detailed here but are known from other sources.
2
Understand Masail al-Usul (Zahir al-Riwayah)
This category includes rulings transmitted from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (and sometimes others) through reliable chains. It is called 'Zahir al-Riwayah' because the transmission is established via trustworthy narrators, either mutawatir or mashhur.
The six books of Imam Muhammad form the core of this category.
3
List the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah
The passage names: Al-Mabsut, Al-Jami' al-Saghir, Al-Jami' al-Kabir, Al-Siyar (which includes Al-Siyar al-Saghir and Al-Siyar al-Kabir), and Al-Ziyadat. These are all authored by Imam Muhammad.
Al-Mabsut is the most comprehensive; its most authoritative copy is by Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani.
4
Note the authority weight
Zahir al-Riwayah holds the highest authority in the Hanafi school for istinbat and fatwa. Later works like Nawadir and Fatawa are secondary and must be consistent with these foundations.
Ibn 'Abidin emphasizes the importance of knowing the various copies and commentaries of Al-Mabsut.

The third study: On the categories of issues
(1)
Al-Kafawi said in 'A'lam al-Akhyar fi Tarjamat al-Imam Muhammad': Know that the issues of our madhhab are of three categories:
The first category: Issues of Usul, which are the issues of Zahir al-Riwayah, and they are the issues of 'Al-Mabsut' by Muhammad, and it has copies, the most famous and prominent of which is the copy of Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani (3), and it is called 'Al-Asl', and the issues of 'Al-Jami' al-Saghir', 'Al-Jami' al-Kabir', 'Al-Siyar', and 'Al-Ziyadat' all are authored by Muhammad. 'Al-Mabsut' has copies: among them the copy of Shaykh al-Islam Abu Bakr, known as Khawhar Zada, and it is called 'Mabsut Shaykh al-Islam' and 'Al-Mabsut al-Kubra'.
(4)
(1) Kata'ib A'lam al-Akhyar (q 73/b) onwards.
(2) And they are issues transmitted from the imams of the madhhab: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, and sometimes joined by Zafar, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, and others who took fiqh from Abu Hanifa. But the prevalent in Zahir al-Riwayah is the statement of the three or some of them. It is called Zahir al-Riwayah because it is transmitted from Muhammad through reliable narrations; thus it is established from him either mutawatir or mashhur. Reference: 'Sharh Rasm al-Mufti' (p. 16), and 'Al-Madkhal' (p. 252).
(3) He is Musa ibn Sulayman al-Juzjani, Abu Sulayman; he took fiqh... 'Al-Saghir', 'Kitab al-Salah', 'Kitab al-Rahn', 'Al-Nawadir'; died after 200 AH. Reference: 'Al-Jawahir' (3:518-519), 'Al-Fawa'id' (p. 354).
(4) Ibn 'Abidin said in Radd al-Muhtar (1:75): Know that the copies of al-Mabsut transmitted from Muhammad are numerous, and the most prominent is the Mabsut of Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani. And al-Mabsut was commented upon by a group of later scholars such as Shaykh al-Islam Abu Bakr known as Khawhar Zada and called 'Al-Mabsut al-Kabir', and Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani and others. Their comments are in reality commentaries, mixed with Muhammad's Mabsut, just as the commentators of al-Jami' al-Saghir did, such as Fakhr al-Islam and Qadikhan and others. So it is said: Qadikhan mentioned in 'Al-Jami' al-Saghir' meaning his commentary. The same applies to others. Summarized from Sharh al-Birri on al-Ashbah, and Sharh al-Shaykh Isma'il al-Nabulusi on Sharh al-Durar. Preserving this is important, like preserving the categories of the mashayikh of the madhhab.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage from al-Kafawi's work defines the three categories of Hanafi masail. The first and most authoritative category is Masail al-Usul, also known as Zahir al-Riwayah. These are the issues found in the six books of Imam Muhammad: Al-Mabsut, Al-Jami' al-Saghir, Al-Jami' al-Kabir, Al-Siyar al-Saghir, Al-Siyar al-Kabir, and Al-Ziyadat. The most reliable copy of Al-Mabsut is that of Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani. The text explains that 'Zahir al-Riwayah' means it is transmitted from Muhammad through trustworthy narrators, establishing it as either mutawatir or mashhur. The later commentary by Ibn 'Abidin notes that multiple copies and commentaries exist, and it is important to keep track of them, just as one must know the layers of the madhhab's scholars.

Key Terms
مسائل الأصول (masa'il al-usul): The foundational issues of the Hanafi madhhab, i.e., the Zahir al-Riwayah.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted issues from Imam Muhammad through reliable chains, as found in his six major works.
المبسوط (al-Mabsut): The comprehensive fiqh work by Imam Muhammad; its most famous copy is by Abu Sulayman al-Juzjani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Al-Mabsut by Imam Muhammad
B) Al-Jami' al-Saghir
C) Al-Hidayah by al-Marghinani
D) Al-Ziyadat by Imam Muhammad

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's most authoritative rulings come from the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah, with Al-Mabsut (Juzjani copy) being the most prominent.

The Three Strata of Hanafi Legal Rulings
Classification of Hanafi sources into three hierarchical strata

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three strata of Hanafi legal sources and identify which stratum a given source belongs to, applying the hierarchy to determine its authority.

Core Idea
Hanafi rulings are not equal; Zahir al-Riwayah is primary, then Ghayr Zahir, and Fatawa only when needed.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya introduces a foundational classification for all Hanafi study: the three strata of legal rulings. Understanding this hierarchy is essential because it tells you how much weight to give a ruling when you encounter it. Not everything attributed to the madhhab carries the same authority. This lesson will walk you through each stratum and its sources, so you can navigate Hanafi texts with confidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first stratum
Read the passage to find what constitutes the highest authority (Zahir al-Riwayah). Note the core books of Imam Muhammad and key derivative texts like al-Muntaqa and al-Kafi.
Zahir al-Riwayah comes from the six well-known books of Shaybani.
2
Identify the second stratum
Find the definition of Ghayr Zahir al-Riwayah. Note that it includes less-known works of Muhammad, books by other scholars like al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, and the Nawadir.
These are less reliable than Zahir al-Riwayah but still part of the school.
3
Identify the third stratum
Locate the description of al-Fatawa wa al-Waqi'at. Understand that these are later ijtihad when no explicit ruling exists from the early imams.
The first such compilation was al-Nawazil by Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi.
4
Apply the hierarchy
Given a source, determine its stratum. For example, if you find a ruling in al-Mabsut, it is Zahir al-Riwayah; in al-Amali, it is Ghayr Zahir; in al-Nawazil, it is third stratum.
This hierarchy guides fatwa practice: rely on higher strata first.

The third study: Strata of the issues. Among them is the copy of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, and the copy of Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, the teacher of al-Sarakhsi. From the issues of Zahir al-Riwayah are the issues of the book 'al-Muntaqa' by al-Hakim al-Shahid, which is a foundation of the school after the books of Muhammad, and it is not found in these eras and regions. (2) And the book 'al-Kafi' by al-Hakim is also from the foundations of the school, and the shaykhs have explained it: among them is the commentary of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. (3) And the commentary of Shaykh al-Islam 'Ali al-Qadi al-Asbijabi. The second stratum: from the issues of the school are the issues of non-Zahir al-Riwayah, which are issues narrated from the imams, but not in the mentioned books: (4) 1. Either in other books of Muhammad, like al-Kaysaniyyat, al-Raqqiyyat, al-Jurjaniyyat, and al-Haruniyyat. It is called non-Zahir al-Riwayah because they are not well-known from Muhammad and were not transmitted from him by the ways of the first books. 2. Or in books not by Muhammad, like 'al-Mujarrad' by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad, and among them the books of 'al-Amali' (dictations). Dictation is that the scholar sits with students around him with inkpots and papers, and the scholar speaks what Allah opens for him of knowledge, and the students write what he says session by session, then they collect what they wrote, so it becomes a book, called al-Amali, and this was the custom of our early companions. 3. And among them: scattered narrations like the narration of Ibn Sam'a and others from the companions of Muhammad, and other issues contradicting the principles, for they are non-Zahir al-Riwayah and counted among the Nawadir: like Nawadir Ibn Sam'a, Nawadir Hisham, and Nawadir Ibn Rustam. The third stratum: al-Fatawa (legal opinions), also called al-Waqi'at, which are issues deduced by the later scholars from the companions of Muhammad and the companions of the companions of Muhammad and those after them in events for which no narration from the three imams is found. The first book compiled in what is known is 'al-Nawazil' authored by the faqih Abu al-Layth Nasr ibn Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Samarqandi, known as Imam al-Huda, in which he collected the fatwas of the later mujtahid scholars from his shaykhs and the shaykhs of his shaykhs, like Muhammad ibn Muqatil al-Razi. Footnotes: (1) These are issues collected by Muhammad for a man named Harun; see al-Madkhal p.254. (2) He is Muhammad ibn Sam'a ibn 'Ubayd Allah al-Tamimi, Abu 'Abd Allah. The reason for Ibn Sam'a writing the Nawadir from Muhammad was that he saw him in a dream as if he was piercing needles, so he interpreted it, and it was said: This is a man who speaks wisdom, so strive that no word from him escapes you. So he began then and wrote the Nawadir from him. Among his works: 'Adab al-Qada' and 'al-Mahadir wa al-Sijillat' (d.233H). See al-Taqrib p.417 and al-Jawahir 3:168-170. (3) He is Hisham ibn 'Ubayd Allah al-Razi. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan died in his house in Rayy and was buried in their cemetery. Among his works: 'al-Nawadir' and 'Salat al-Athar'. He said: I met one thousand seven hundred shaykhs and spent seven hundred thousand dirhams on knowledge. See al-Jawahir 3:569-570, Tabaqat al-Hanafiyya p.28, and al-Fawa'id p.364. (4) He is Ibrahim ibn Rustam al-Marwazi, Abu Bakr. He studied under Muhammad and narrated from Nuh al-Jami' and heard from Malik (d.211H). See al-Fawa'id p.27. (5) He is Muhammad ibn Muqatil al-Razi, from the companions of Muhammad, judge of Rayy (d.248H). See al-Jawahir 3:273, al-Fawa'id p.329, and al-Taqrib p.442.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents a systematic classification of Hanafi legal sources into three strata, which is essential for understanding the hierarchy of authority within the madhhab. The author begins with the first stratum, Zahir al-Riwayah, which consists of the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and their derivatives like al-Muntaqa and al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid. These are the most authoritative sources. Commentaries on these works, such as those by al-Sarakhsi and al-Asbijabi, are also part of this stratum. The second stratum comprises non-Zahir al-Riwayah, which includes rulings transmitted from the imams but through less reliable chains or in less-known works. Examples are al-Kaysaniyyat, al-Raqqiyyat, al-Jurjaniyyat, and al-Haruniyyat from Imam Muhammad himself, as well as works by other scholars like al-Mujarrad by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and the al-Amali (dictations). Also included are the Nawadir (rare narrations) by Ibn Sam'a, Hisham, and Ibn Rustam. These carry less weight than Zahir al-Riwayah. The third stratum, al-Fatawa or al-Waqi'at, consists of legal opinions deduced by later scholars when no explicit narration from the three founding imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is found. The first compilation of such was al-Nawazil by Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi. This classification helps students understand that not all Hanafi rulings have the same authority, and that later fatwas are only used when primary texts are silent.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah): The most authoritative stratum of Hanafi rulings, transmitted through reliable chains in the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and their commentaries.
غير ظاهر الرواية (Ghayr Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah): Rulings transmitted from the imams but through less reliable or less well-known chains, including those in non-standard collections or from other companions.
الفتاوى والواقعات (al-Fatāwā wa al-Wāqi‘āt): Legal opinions derived by later Hanafi jurists for new situations not explicitly covered in earlier texts; the third stratum of authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which book is the first known compilation of the third stratum (fatawa/wāqi‘āt)?
A) al-Muntaqa by al-Hakim al-Shahid
B) al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid
C) al-Nawazil by Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi
D) al-Mujarrad by al-Hasan ibn Ziyad

Key Takeaway
Hanafi rulings are organized into three strata; use the hierarchy to determine which source takes precedence in fatwa.

Method of Learning

The Hierarchy of Hanafi Texts: Mutun Over Shuruh Over Fatawa
Establishes priority rule across Hanafi textual layers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchical priority rule for Hanafi legal sources and apply it to a scenario where a matn conflicts with a fatwa collection.

Core Idea
In the Hanafi school, matns (core texts) take precedence over commentaries and fatwa collections.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah establishes a fundamental methodological principle for navigating Hanafi legal literature. When faced with conflicting rulings, the scholar must know which source carries greater authority. The rule is clear: priority is given first to the matn, then to the sharh, and finally to the fatwa collections. This ensures consistency and preserves the school's authentic positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three textual layers
Distinguish between mutun (core texts like Mukhtasar al-Quduri), shuruh (commentaries like al-Hidayah), and fatawa (fatwa collections like al-Khulasah). Understand the nature and purpose of each.
The matn is the foundational text; the sharh expands on it; the fatwa applies rulings to new cases.
2
State the priority rule
The passage states clearly: mutun are given precedence over shuruh, and shuruh over fatawa. Memorize this hierarchy.
This is a general rule; exceptions exist.
3
Recognize the sources of the rule
The author quotes multiple Hanafi authorities: al-Shurnbulali, al-Hamawi, al-Tartusi, Ibn Abidin, Ibn Nujaym, al-Haskafi, al-Timurtashi. Each affirms the same principle.
These quotes serve as the proof (dalil) for the rule within the madhhab.
4
Apply the rule to a conflict scenario
Suppose a matn (e.g., Kanz al-Daqa'iq) says a certain sale is valid, but a later fatwa (e.g., al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya) says it is invalid. According to the rule, the matn's ruling should be followed.
The fatwa does not override the matn unless there is explicit correction (see Lesson 2).
5
Note the limitation of the rule
The hierarchy applies when there is no explicit taṣḥīḥ (correction) stating otherwise. If a later source explicitly corrects the matn, that may take precedence (covered in Lesson 2).
Also, the Imam's own statement always takes precedence over others.

الدراسة الثالثة: طبقات المسائل ومحمد بن سلمة ونصير بن يحيى، وذكر فيها اختياراته أيضاً، وهو أصل الواقعات. ثم جمع المشايخ فيه كتباً: كـ«مجموع النوازل والواقعات» للنَّاطِفِي والصدر الشهيد وغيره. ثمَّ جمع من بعدهم من المشايخ هذه الطبقات في فتاواهم مختلطة غير ممتازة، كما في «جامع قاضي خان»، و«الخلاصة»، وغيرهما من كتب الفتاوى، وقد ميّز بعضُهم كما في «المحيط» لرضي الدين السِّرَخْسِي فإنه بدأ بمسائل الأصول أولاً، ثم النوادر ثم الفتاوى. انتهى كلامه. وقد ذكرتُ بعض ما يتعلّق بهذا البحث في «مقدمة الهداية»، وفي «النافع الكبير لمن يطالع الجامع الصغير»، فليرجع إليهما.
واعلم أنهم ذكروا عمدة الرعاية على شرح الوقاية أي إن كبار علماء المذهب نصوا على ذلك، ومن أقوالهم ما يلي: قال الشرنبلالي في حاشيته على الدرر: ونحفظ عن مشايخنا تقديم ما في المتون والشروح على ما في الفتاوى. وقال الحموي في «غمز العيون»: العمل على ما في المتون إذا عارضه ما في الفتاوى. وقال الطرطوسي في «أنفع الوسائل»: إذا تعارض تصحيح ما في المتون والفتاوى فالمعتمد ما في المتون. انتهى. ينظر: غمز العيون (٤: ١٥٥). وقال ابن عابدين في رد المحتار: إذا اختلف التصحيح لقولين وكان أحدهما قول الإمام أو في المتون أخذ بما هو قول الإمام؛ لأنه صاحب المذهب، وبما في المتون؛ لأنها موضوعة لنقل المذهب. وقال فيه: متى اختلف الترجيح رجح ما في المتون. وقال فيه أيضاً: والمتون مقدمة على الشروح. وقال فيه أيضاً: في قضاء الفوائت من البحر أنه إذا اختلف التصحيح والفتوى فالعمل بما وافق المتون أولى. انتهى. وكذا لو كان أحدهما في الشروح والآخر في الفتاوى لما صرحوا به من أن ما في المتون مقدم على ما في الشروح وما في الشروح مقدم على ما في الفتاوى، لكن هذا عند التصريح بتصحيح كل من القولين أو عدم التصريح أصلاً. أما لو ذكرت مسألة المتون لم يصرحوا بتصحيحها بل صرحوا بتصحيح مقابلها، فقد أفاد العلامة قاسم ترجيح الثاني؛ لأنه تصحيح صريح وما في المتون تصحيح التزامي، والتصحيح الصريح مقدم على التصحيح الالتزامي: أي التزام المتون ذكر ما هو الصحيح في المذهب. وكذا لا تخيير لو كان أحدهما قول الإمام والآخر قول غيره؛ لأنه لما تعارض التصحيحان تساقطا، فرجعنا إلى الأصل وهو تقديم قول الإمام. بل في شهادات الفتاوى الخيرية: المقرر عندنا أنه لا يفتى ويعمل إلا بقول الإمام الأعظم، ولا يعدل عنه إلى قولهما أو قول أحدهما أو غيرهما إلا لضرورة كمسألة المزارعة، وإن صرح المشايخ بأن الفتوى على قولهما؛ لأنه صاحب المذهب والإمام المقدم. انتهى. ومثله في البحر عند الكلام على أوقات الصلاة. وفيه من كتاب القضاء: يحل الإفتاء بقول الإمام، بل يجب وإن لم يعلم من أين قال. انتهى. وقال ابن نجيم في البحر الرائق: العمل على ما هو في المتون؛ لأنه إذا تعارض ما في المتون والفتاوى فالمعتمد ما في المتون، وكذا يقدم ما في الشروح على ما في الفتاوى. وقال فيه: والإفتاء بما في المتون أولى. وقال الحصكفي في الدر المنتقى: والإفتاء بما في المتون أولى. وقال في الدر المختار: ورجح في الشرنبلالية بأن ما في المتون والشروح أولى بما في كتب الفتاوى فليحفظ. وقال التمرتاشي في منح الغفار: إن اختلف التصحيح، فالمعتمد ما في المتون.
The third study: Layers of legal issues, and Muhammad ibn Salamah and Nasir ibn Yahya, and he mentioned therein his selections also, and it is the origin of incidents. Then the scholars compiled books on it: such as 'Majmu' al-Nawazil wa al-Waqi'at' by al-Natifi and al-Sadr al-Shahid and others. Then the later scholars compiled these layers in their fatwas mixed without distinction, as in 'Jami' Qadi Khan' and 'al-Khulasah' and other books of fatwas, and some distinguished as in 'al-Muhit' by Radi al-Din al-Sarakhsi, for he began with issues of the principles first, then the rare reports, then the fatwas. His speech ends. And I have mentioned some of what pertains to this research in 'Muqaddimat al-Hidayah' and in 'al-Nafi' al-Kabir li man Yutali' al-Jami' al-Saghir', so refer to them. And know that they mentioned in 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah 'ala Sharh al-Wiqayah' that the senior scholars of the school stated that, and from their statements are the following: Al-Shurnbalali said in his gloss on al-Durar: We have preserved from our teachers the precedence of what is in the matns and commentaries over what is in the fatwas. And al-Hamawi said in 'Ghamz al-'Uyun': The practice is upon what is in the matns when what is in the fatwas contradicts it. And al-Tartusi said in 'Anfa' al-Wasa'il': If there is a conflict in verification between what is in the matns and the fatwas, the relied-upon is what is in the matns. End. See: Ghamz al-'Uyun (4:155). And Ibn 'Abidin said in Radd al-Muhtar: If verification differs on two statements and one of them is the Imam's statement or is in the matns, one takes what is the Imam's statement, because he is the master of the school, and what is in the matns, because they are established to transmit the school. And he said in it: Whenever the preference differs, prefer what is in the matns. And he also said in it: The matns are given precedence over the commentaries. And he also said in it: In 'Qada' al-Fawa'it' from al-Bahr, that if verification and fatwa differ, then practice in accordance with what agrees with the matns is better. End. And likewise if one is in the commentaries and the other in the fatwas, because they stated that what is in the matns is given precedence over what is in the commentaries, and what is in the commentaries is given precedence over what is in the fatwas. But this is when explicit verification of each of the two statements is given, or no verification at all. As for if the matn mentions an issue without explicit verification, but they explicitly verify its opposite, then al-'Allamah Qasim stated the preference of the second, because it is explicit verification and what is in the matn is implicit verification, and explicit verification takes precedence over implicit verification: i.e., the matn's implicit undertaking to mention what is correct in the school. And likewise there is no option if one is the Imam's statement and the other is another's statement, because when verifications conflict they cancel out, so we return to the principle which is giving precedence to the Imam's statement. Rather, in 'Shahadat' of al-Fatawa al-Khayriyyah: The established view with us is that no fatwa is given nor practice carried out except by the statement of the Great Imam, and one does not deviate from it to their statement or the statement of one of them or others except for necessity, like the issue of muzara'ah, even if the scholars explicitly state that the fatwa is upon their statement, because he is the master of the school and the leading Imam. End. And similarly in al-Bahr when discussing the times of prayer. And in it from the Book of Judgments: It is permissible to give fatwa by the Imam's statement, rather it is obligatory even if one does not know from where he said it. End. And Ibn Nujaym said in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: Practice is upon what is in the matns, because if what is in the matns and the fatwas conflict, the relied-upon is what is in the matns, and likewise what is in the commentaries is given precedence over what is in the fatwas. And he said in it: And giving fatwa by what is in the matns is better. And al-Haskafi said in al-Durr al-Muntaqa: And giving fatwa by what is in the matns is better. And he said in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: And it was preferred in al-Shurnabulaliyyah that what is in the matns and commentaries is better than what is in the fatwa books, so let it be memorized. And al-Timurtashi said in Minah al-Ghaffar: If verification differs, the relied-upon is what is in the matns.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage covers a foundational methodological principle in the Hanafi school: the hierarchy of textual sources. The author first introduces the concept of 'layers' (tabaqāt) of legal issues, explaining how early scholars like Muhammad ibn Salamah and Nasir ibn Yahya compiled the foundational texts (uṣūl) and rare reports (nawādir), and how later scholars mixed these layers in their fatwa collections. He then presents the critical rule: when there is a conflict, priority is given first to the matn (core text), then to the sharḥ (commentary), and finally to fatwa collections. He supports this with numerous quotes from leading Hanafi jurists: al-Shurnbulālī, al-Ḥamawī, al-Ṭarṭūsī, Ibn ʿĀbidīn, Ibn Nujaym, al-Ḥaṣkafī, al-Tīmurtāshī, and others. An important nuance is added by al-ʿAllāmah Qāsim: if a matn mentions a ruling implicitly without explicit correction, but a fatwa explicitly corrects the opposite, the explicit correction takes precedence because it is explicit versus implicit. Furthermore, if one statement is from Imam Abū Ḥanīfa and another from a different imam, the Imam's word prevails, as he is the master of the school. This hierarchy is not absolute; necessity may require deviating from the Imam's view, as in the case of muzāraʿah (sharecropping). The passage also notes that giving fatwa by the Imam's statement is not only permissible but sometimes obligatory, even without knowing the exact evidence. This rule ensures consistency and authority in the school's legal practice.

Key Terms
متون (mutūn): The core primary texts of the Hanafi school, such as Mukhtaṣar al-Qudūrī and Kanz al-Daqāʾiq, which are the foundational repositories of the school's rulings.
شروح (shurūḥ): Commentaries written upon the core texts (mutūn), such as al-Hidāyah or al-ʿInāyah, which explain, expand, and sometimes reconcile differences within the school.
فتاوى (fatāwā): Collections of legal opinions (fatwas) by later scholars, which often compile rulings from various sources and may indicate the preferred view in practice.
تصحيح (taṣḥīḥ): The process of establishing which among conflicting opinions is the correct (ṣaḥīḥ) view for the school. It can be explicit (ṣarīḥ) or implicit (iltizāmī) when the matn adopts a particular view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi textual hierarchy, if a commentary (sharh) says one thing and a fatwa collection says another, which source is given precedence?
A) The commentary.
B) The fatwa collection.
C) Both are equal.
D) It depends on the scholar.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when sources conflict, the order of priority is: matn, then sharh, then fatwa collection—a rule that preserves the school's authentic positions.

Method of Learning
